## Thread: Call yourselfa Programmer? — 12 this month / 14 total

### Call yourselfa Programmer?

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
This is one of the best written blog entries I have read for a long time.

It is a bit long for a Blog but it is very pertinent and there are some 
pearls of wisdom in amongst the beads of humour.

Enjoy!

http://www.kalzumeus.com/2011/10/28/dont-call-yourself-a-programmer/

Pete.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Call yourselfa Programmer?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-01T10:57:35+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9h8juhFle2U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9h7hqgFj9iU1@mid.individual.net> <u2cta7l7t8gm2o77r7aftaq8i5ej87b2r5@4ax.com>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Tue, 1 Nov 2011 01:15:10 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> Regards,


Good and fair comments, Steve.

I think perhaps you are taking it a little seriously, though.

The guy has 10 years as a software Engineer. (not to mention another 6 years 
in Acadaemia where he confesses being moved to tears by frustration on at 
least one occasion.)

Obviously, compared to people who post here, 15 years or so isn't much, but 
he is representative of the new breed and I really liked his sense of 
humour.

The FACT is that "programming" today covers a multitude of sins and it isn't 
as straughtforward and clear-cut as it was in the days when programmers cut 
code in Assembler or COBOL.

During the latter part of my career I was required to interview many 
candidates for programming teams. Some of them were very bright young people 
making a career in programming who had every right to call themselves 
"programmers". But there were equally as many who couldn't pass the FizzBuzz 
test.

I should think that every Assembler programmer knows the old XOR trick for 
swapping fields without using intermediate storage, but I wouldn't mind 
betting there are people reading this who couldn't do it in COBOL.

The fact is that "programming" is being deskilled and is being used to cover 
much more than it was in Ancient Tymes.

Maybe "Engineering" IS a better word...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Call yourselfa Programmer?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-03T01:04:30+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9hcpugF3pgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9h7hqgFj9iU1@mid.individual.net> <u2cta7l7t8gm2o77r7aftaq8i5ej87b2r5@4ax.com> <9h8juhFle2U1@mid.individual.net> <u7v0b79k7ftc103do1u93ba627tc5agjs6@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Tue, 1 Nov 2011 10:57:35 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>> Pete.

Thanks Clark, for 'fessing up... (in which I see no dishonour, BTW) :-)

It isn't something COBOL people would be called upon to do very often.

Consider the following:

01 numeric-fields.
     12 fld-A pic 9(8) value 12345678.
     12 fld-B pic 9(8) value  87654321..

*> swap the 2 elemental fields without using any other storage...
                                                         fld-A 
fld-B
                                                     12345678      87654321
      subtract  fld-A from fld-B        12345678      75308643
      add        f ld-B to     fld-A        87654321     75308643
      subtract fld-A  from  fld-B        87654321     12345678

The actual result in fld-B would be negative, so the fields need to be 
unsigned.

All very well for numbers, but what about Alpha characters?

There are several ways I can think of for going about this, but none of them 
are really elegant and they all require REDEFINES. If anybody has an elegant 
solution please post it for the sake of completeness and our edification :-)

If it were my problem I would revert to using Boolean functions (AND, OR, 
NOT, and XOR) on the two strings. But that presupposes your compiler 
supports those functions (both MicroFocus and Fujitsu COBOLs do) and it is 
really an Assembler solution dressed up clumsily in COBOL.

Fortunately, in this day and age we don't have to even think about 
conserving storage in this way and it is just an intellectual exercise.

Pete.
```

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-12T15:11:15-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3231e30a-90c2-462f-a00a-f28236a32ffb@hh9g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9h7hqgFj9iU1@mid.individual.net> <u2cta7l7t8gm2o77r7aftaq8i5ej87b2r5@4ax.com> <9h8juhFle2U1@mid.individual.net> <u7v0b79k7ftc103do1u93ba627tc5agjs6@4ax.com> <9hcpugF3pgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Nov 2, 7:04 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> All very well for numbers, but what about Alpha characters?
>
> There are several ways I can think of for going about this, but none of them
> are really elegant and they all require REDEFINES. If anybody has an elegant
> solution please post it for the sake of completeness and our edification :-)

=====
       identification division.
       program-id. swap.
       data division.
       01 numeric-field.
           12 field-1 pic 9(8) value 12345678.
           12 field-2 pic 9(8) value 56781234.
       01 alpha-field.
           12 field-a pic x(8) value "abcdefgh".
           12 field-b pic x(8) value "ijklmnop".
       procedure division.
       begin.
           display "numeric"
           display field-1 space field-2
           move function reverse (numeric-field)
               to numeric-field
           move function reverse (field-1 (1:)) to field-1 (1:)
           move function reverse (field-2 (1:)) to field-2 (1:)
           display field-1 space field-2
           display space
           display "alpha"
           display field-a space field-b
           move function reverse (alpha-field)
               to alpha-field
           move function reverse (field-a) to field-a
           move function reverse (field-b) to field-b
           display field-a space field-b
           stop run
           .
=====
numeric
12345678 56781234
56781234 12345678

alpha
abcdefgh ijklmnop
ijklmnop abcdefgh
=====
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ ODO with Intrinsic Functions (was Re: 77 Levels)

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-02T12:25:40-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<33503ae4-31d5-4e81-9bd4-68ac2e08753c@n18g2000vbv.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <a35c4935-d3b0-4730-9c2c-cbc823e9774d@h30g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <9gmaolFmhU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Oct 24, 6:30 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> I would strongly argue that people shouldn't use OCCURS ...DEPENDING ON, for
> instance, but not that they shouldn't use 77s :-)

ISO/IEC 1989:2002
15.2 Arguments (Intrinsic functions)
"When the definition of a function permits an argument
to be repeated a variable number of times, a table may
be referenced by specifying the data-name and any
qualifiers that identify the table, followed immediately
by subscripting where one or more of the subscripts is
the word ALL.

"When ALL is specified as a subscript, the effect is as
if each table element associated with that subscript
position were specified. ... If the ALL subscript is
associated with a data item described with an OCCURS
DEPENDING ON clause, the range of values is determined
by the object of the OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause."

Intrinsic functions that can accept ALL table elements as
arguments (2002 and ANSI X3.23a-1989): MAX, MEAN, MEDIAN,
MIDRANGE, MIN, ORD-MAX, ORD-MIN, PRESENT-VALUE, RANGE,
STANDARD-DEVIATION, SUM, and VARIANCE.

For those functions, SEARCH, SEARCH ALL, and SORT (2002);
OCCURS DEPENDING ON simplifies the programming task.
It follows that, where a table may contain a variable
number of elements, the use of OCCURS DEPENDING ON should
be encouraged.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Mon, 21 Nov 2011 14:13:42 -0800 (PST), "john@wexfordpress.com"
> <john@wexfordpress.com> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> stored next to that table, and never used for any other table.

Yes, I do that too, inasmuch as I always use INDEXED BY and provide the 
required index as a unique index used ONLY by THAT table.


Most of my programs have a little definition in WORKING-STORAGE as follows:

01   subscripts usage comp-5.
      12 J                  pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 K                pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 L                 pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 M                pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 N                pic s9(4) value zero.

These work as general purpose registers and get used for things like 
intermediate calculations in string manipulation and reference modification.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-23T14:50:34+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Tue, 22 Nov 2011 02:48:04 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> is a whole number.

comp-5 is little endian, as per x86 archtecture. You could just as easily 
make it comp, which IS valid in IBM COBOL.
>
> And Id be fired if I used subscripts with names like that!!

I'm sorry to hear that. I suggest that that would be their loss... :-) Hard 
to imagine that programmers couldn't deal with it, or that Site Management 
would be so rigid in adherence to a concept like "readability", when what 
they really mean is "verbosity". The letters are no harder to read than 
using blah-blah-sub. I have worked on sites where it was the site standard 
for subscript use in COBOL.

The point about them is that they have no fixed use (unlike an INDEX defined 
for a specific table), so you know that you cannot just expect them to 
contain the "right" value; they must be initialised before each use. (I use 
them a lot with PERFORM... VARYING and that automatically sets an initial 
value. I also find them useful for STRING and UNSTRING and INSPECT as 
Pointers and tallies. In fact just about anywhere you need an integer...). 
As soon as you see one in my code you KNOW it is NOT attached to any 
particular array or table and is just a local count or pointer for the 
construct where it is being used.

Please understand, I'm not advocating everyone should do this, I just 
mentioned it in passing. It is something I found useful during the decades 
when I wrote COBOL for a living. (And I first did it on IBM mainframes.)

Pete.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<0kfpc7171lllj4j4k993p2s2kfaleuhphb@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 14:50:34 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Tue, 22 Nov 2011 02:48:04 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
…[47 more quoted lines elided]…
>when I wrote COBOL for a living. (And I first did it on IBM mainframes.)


I'd have to say that using the one character subscript/counter names
is a bad idea in Cobol.  They make absolute sense, and *add* to
clarity in more modular languages with local variables*.  A locally
scoped variable named, i, j, k, etc. in those languages is
idiomatically recognized as a a loop counter/subscript, with very
local usage and without the visual clutter of long variable names.

But it's bad if those have global scope, since they are no longer
contained, and have the same problems any variables with global scope
have.  Just consider the issues of one routine with a loop calling a
subroutine with a loop - you had darn well better not use i as a loop
counter in both.

The lack of proper subroutines (pretty much you need parameters and
local variables) is one of Cobol's greatest failings, and while it
more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
perfectly clear that Cobol was horribly deficient in that area.
Basically original Fortran came close, and languages like Algol had
the whole boat no later than the mid-sixties.

As to indexes, those are another Cobol mistake.  Indexes made (some)
sense in the context of dumb compilers, much like the old Fortran
subscripting rules**, although the Fortran limitations were fairly
quickly eased.


*admittedly OO Cobol goes a fair way to providing that, and in that
case I have no objections, although that's certainly not anything like
typical Cobol usage

**Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that.  The point
was that those would be easy forms for the compiler to generate
efficient code.  Obviously that was quickly expanded to approximately
"any expression resulting in an integer."  Note that Fortran never had
a construct like indexes for performance reasons, even though the
application domain for Fortran made *much* heavier use of arrays than
Cobol applications did.
```

On Nov 23, 10:59 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:

> >The point about them is that they have no fixed use (unlike an INDEX defined
> >for a specific table), so you know that you cannot just expect them to
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> counter in both.

I completely agree with that. Peter's 'no fixed use' of global
iterators is poor practice. If there is a PERFORM loop using one of
the variables and within that there is a PERFORM paragraph then there
is nothing that will detect or prevent re-use of the variable at some
point.


> The lack of proper subroutines (pretty much you need parameters and
> local variables) is one of Cobol's greatest failings,

Complete nonsense. CALL / EXIT PROGRAM has been in COBOL since '74 and
was implemented by many vendors in slightly different ways in the 60s.
In ICL 1900 COBOL it was done with "ENTER language NAME" which would
call COBOL, FORTRAN or PLAN routines with parameters. The '74 compiler
used standard CALL.

Nested subroutines were in '85 COBOL, maybe you never got the memo.

> and while it
> more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> quickly eased.

Please explain why they were a "mistake".

> typical Cobol usage

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cobol applications did.

Your claim is ambiguous: Is it:
   a)   "for performance reasons FORTRAN never had indexes"
or b)   "Fortran never had ( indexes for performance )"

In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a6mqc796vj45gau745s2hsi33drer5oir4@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net> <0kfpc7171lllj4j4k993p2s2kfaleuhphb@4ax.com> <733d570d-92fe-4a5f-a807-47c6eee7afce@d37g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 09:48:49 -0800 (PST), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 23, 10:59ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Nested subroutines were in '85 COBOL, maybe you never got the memo.


Subprograms, nested or otherwise, were largely unusable as general
subroutines.  Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
see anyone write a program with no performs, just calls to nested
subprograms?  Obviously possible, but not something most people wopuld
have considered practical.  The lack of typing and a very expensive
call mechanism didn't help either.  And while not a direct fault of
the language, by the time Cobol-85 features became available, much of
the Cobol community had ossified into its final "we've always done
things this way" form.

And yes I got the memo, and got my hand smacked for using "weird stuff
like that."


>> and while it
>> more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Please explain why they were a "mistake".


Indexes added nothing to the expressiveness of than language (some
minor application to SEARCH aside), added considerable complexity to
use (this variable I can modify with an ADD, but *that* I need to use
SET on, and if I want to use the same index value on two arrays, I
have to maintain two separate indexes), and provided only a modest
amount of optimization support for the compiler anyway.

Most decent compilers are quite capable of optimizing most of the
subscripting to be equivalent of what you'd hope a index would
generate, without putting the additional burden on the programmer.  My
point with the Fortran example was that if the Fortran guys (or
frankly anyone else in programming language history) didn't find any
use for such a construct, and the Fortran guys were much more into
processing arrays than the Cobol community, and much bigger on CPU
performance (Cobol users typically did much more I/O, and while CPU
performance was not unimportant, it was less of a focus than for the
Fortran community).

Honestly, for a language intended to provide clarity to programmers
above all else, exposing a trivial micro-optimization like indexes is
just bizarre.


>> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
>> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that. ï¿½The point
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>or b)   "Fortran never had ( indexes for performance )"


The Fortran community never implemented a mechanism such as indexes,
despite being considerably more interested in array access performance
than the Cobol folks.  Basic subscripting (with some initial
restrictions on the form of the subscript expression*), and elementary
optimization techniques, were good enough, and didn't require mucking
up the language.

Some languages that support pointers allow you to produce roughly
equivalent code (incrementing a pointer in C is much like "SET
INDEXNAME UP BY 1" in what it implies for the code), but pointers
wander into a lot of different areas as well.


*Cobol of course having a far greater restrictions on subscript/index
expressions.


>In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
>etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.


Yes, structures were not in original Fortran, although they were added
soon enough.  But that has only limited impact on the subscripting
problem.
```

```
On Nov 24, 10:02 am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 09:48:49 -0800 (PST), Richard
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> subroutines.

No, wrong. They were perfectly usable. It may well be that you did not
use them, but that is not the fault of the language.


> Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
> see anyone write a program with no performs, just calls to nested
> subprograms?  Obviously possible, but not something most people wopuld
> have considered practical.

Where is the requirement that they stop using PERFORMs in order to use
CALLs or vice versa? Both can be used in the same program. Or do you
still use GO TO for program structure ?

> The lack of typing and a very expensive
> call mechanism didn't help either.

COBOL can use dynamic CALLs or it can use static linked CALLs. Nested
program CALLs may well be less expensive than external CALLs but these
are not significantly more costly than similar features in other
languages.


> And while not a direct fault of
> the language, by the time Cobol-85 features became available, much of
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> like that."

Your original point was "COBOL's failings", now we find that they are
in fact programmer's failings, or programmer management failings.


> >> and while it
> >> more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> amount of optimization support for the compiler anyway.

Indexes did not remove subscripts. If you found them confusing then
you had the option of not using them.

They did, in fact, generally improve efficiency, and provide better
error detection. They were not a "mistake", although it seems there
was a 'mistake' in expecting ossified programmers to drag themselves
out of the 60s.

If you didn't find a better performance on array access intensive
programs then you were probably doing it wrong. One commenter wrote:

"""| using binary data items (COMP) to address a table is 30% slower
than using indexes
| using packed decimal data items (COMP-3) to address a table is 300%
slower than using indexes
| using DISPLAY data items to address a table is 450% slower than
using indexes"""


> Most decent compilers are quite capable of optimizing most of the
> subscripting to be equivalent of what you'd hope a index would
> generate, without putting the additional burden on the programmer.

That still does not make index a "mistake".

> My
> point with the Fortran example was that if the Fortran guys (or
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> just bizarre.

Programmers are supposed to make informed decisions about which
constructs to use in particular situations. Indexes are not a 'trivial
micro-optimization', they are also a mechanism for ensuring
correctness by eliminating common programming faults. But it seems
that you never actually discovered where they could be beneficial.

Also I do not see that they reduce clarity, in fact they can enhance
it.


> >> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
> >> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that.  The point
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> up the language.

Because FORTRAN originally had only a limited set of array element
sizes.

> Some languages that support pointers allow you to produce roughly
> equivalent code (incrementing a pointer in C is much like "SET
> INDEXNAME UP BY 1" in what it implies for the code), but pointers
> wander into a lot of different areas as well.

Depending on implemntation of course, yes a 'SET index UP BY 1' could
be equivalent to 'sptr += sizeof(s)' or indeed 'sptr++'. C programmers
find that is quite clear and use it because it is more efficient.

But if you get confused then don't use it, it may have been a
"mistake" to remove subscripting but they didn't. They added more
modern features.


> *Cobol of course having a far greater restrictions on subscript/index
> expressions.

Which is a _good_ thing. C programmers are known to abuse pointers and
gave them a bad name (which is why many modern languages don't have
explicit pointers). COBOL avoided allowing abuse. That was not a
"mistake".


> >In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
> >etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> problem.

"Soon enough" was Fortran90. More than a quarter century after the
original. Perhaps that is how long ossified programmers take to get
their head around a new idea.
```

And it (call mechanism) is hardly "expensive".  It translates in the
Assembler to the standard calling mechanism between programs.  I.E.,
saved area address in register 13, parameter start address in register
1, subroutine address in register 14 and next instruction in calling
program address in register 15.  That standard has been in place for
many, many moons.  I'd say it has paid for itself long ago.

>
>> And while not a direct fault of
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…

…[21 more quoted lines elided]…

My thoughts exactly.  Yes you have to use the SET verb or increment or
decrement a field defined by INDEXED BY.  However, you don't have to
use that field.  You can define your own subscribt in working storage
and use it in that table and use ADD, SUBTRACT, MULTIPLY whatever on
it.  Again, lack of understanding of the language is not a failing of
the language.

>They did, in fact, generally improve efficiency, and provide better

…[99 more quoted lines elided]…
>

About Fortran....I once supported a teller processing system that was
written entirely in Fortran.  The source was compiled on the mainframe
and the object downloaded to the teller station control unit.  The
system built screens and processed them and communicated between the
controller which communicted with the mainframe.  Why the authors
chose Fortran instead of Assmbler is beyond me, but it did show that
Fortran had more uses than just statistics and table manipulation.

SkippyPB wrote:

> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 16:59:00 -0800 (PST), Richard
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> many, many moons.  I'd say it has paid for itself long ago.

The call of a module like
         L     R15,=A(&MODUL)      *
         BASR  R14,R15             *
and the entry code of the module
         USING *,R15
         STM   R14,R12,12(R13)     * SAVE REGISTERS. 
and the exit code of the module
         LM    R14,R12,12(R13)     *
         BR    R14
are AFAIK not more expensive than the equivalence for PERFORM.

> About Fortran....I once supported a teller processing system that was
> written entirely in Fortran.  The source was compiled on the mainframe
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Fortran had more uses than just statistics and table manipulation.

Once a user of a mail monitoring package (written in FORTRAN) noticed it
didn't run very efficiently. So we wrote a prototype of a FORTRAN
compiler in COBOL which gained them 50% of the CPU time (and the need
to buy an additional mainframe). See the example at 
http://fred.mobach.nl/download/forass.cbl.gz
```

```
On Wed, 30 Nov 2011 02:29:00 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Robert Wessel wrote:
><snipped>>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>I'd be interested to know.


We used an approach a number of other folks used for HLL TSR support.
Basically an (assembler) library which when called would install the
running program as a TSR, set up all the interrupt vectors that needed
to be shelled, and whatnot.  It also watched for the triggering
events, which were appropriately set from the Cobol program.

The application itself operated in a polled mode.  When it decided to
go to sleep, it would call "TSR_WAIT", and the library would return
control to the foreground program.  When a waking event occurred, it
was initially handled by the library, which finished by switching
stacks and then returning to the Cobol application from the TSR_WAIT
call.  The application could then check to see exactly what event
caused the wakeup.

The application could also check for events that occurred while it was
active.

So there was no direct interrupt handling or that sort of thing in the
Cobol program, and all of the weirdness happened outside.  With a few
exceptions (memory allocation and program invocations, for example),
this mostly worked as normal.

Additional support was in the TSR library to save and restore the
screen state, although our screen management library did most of that
already, it just needed to do it at odd times (basically if the Cobol
application wanted to hit the screen, say popping up after a hotkey,
it would call TSR_SAVE_SCREEEN, SCREENER_INIT, ...interact...,
SCREENER_CLOSE, TSR_RESTORE_SCREEN - and only the first and last of
those was actually new).  And a few other things like that

Realia's contribution was more an avoidance of bad behavior when
interfacing with DOS and the BIOS.
```

On Mon, 28 Nov 2011 23:29:34 -0800 (PST), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 29, 6:06ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 16:59:00 -0800 (PST), Richard
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>Please feel free to use whatever language that you find non-ugly.


At least with a perform, you can access the defined structure from all
the routines.  You need to redescribe the data areas with a (Cobol-85)
subprogram.


>> >>ï¿½Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
>> >> see anyone write a program with no performs, just calls to nested
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>Even a statically linked non-nested CALL needs a fixup.


Statically linked subprograms, whether nested or "conventional,"
typically use the same calling convention, perhaps plus or minus a
smidge.  Dynamically linked subprograms usually have a bit more
overhead.  All of those calling conventions are typically quite
expensive.

>> >> And while not a direct fault of
>> >> the language, by the time Cobol-85 features became available, much of
…[56 more quoted lines elided]…
>different constraints.


Very little different.  We're talking about arrays.  The Fortran guys,
who care *a lot* more about array performance (especially since array
elements are often very small - a single scalar, in the case of
classic Fortran), did not see fit to introduce an alternate mechanism
for indexing, just to improve performance.

The compilation techniques for optimizing array accesses are very,
very old.  In fact strength reduction and loop invariant hoisting,
were implemented in some of the earliest Fortran compilers.  To answer
you question below, making that easier was the motivation for the
restricted forms of subscript expressions in the original Fortran.
They quickly discovered that they didn't need those restrictions, and
eliminated them.

And if I've harped too much on Fortran, these were common
optimizations in Algol as well.


>> >> Most decent compilers are quite capable of optimizing most of the
>> >> subscripting to be equivalent of what you'd hope a index would
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>are simply puffery.


For whatever reason, with or without optimization the code emitted
from many Cobol compilers is terrible (despite vendor claims to the
contrary).  Certainly the vendors of some of the most important
compilers have rarely, if ever, had any motivation to improve them.


>> >>ï¿½My
>> >> point with the Fortran example was that if the Fortran guys (or
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>limitations.


Yep, limitations are certainly on display here...


>> >> >> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
>> >> >> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that. ï¿½The point
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
>also of subscript checks being off.


Hardly.  If subscript range checks are a feature of the language, they
probably need to be implemented by the compiler.  With most Cobol
compilers set to "optimize," they're not.  So you can't claim that
(say) C is worse because it doesn't have range checks, if your running
you Cobol code without the range checks turned on.  And the notion
that you only have them on during development is silly - almost all of
the buffer overflows that happen to C programs didn't happen during
testing, just with peculiar, often malicious, input data.

While not impacting the sixties and seventies, it's now pretty clear
that you can optimize much of the work of range checks away (obviously
you need a language that preserves enough size information), so that
they add very little overhead.  For example, if you have a loop, if
you can check the range of the loop, you can often determine that
you'll never generate a bad (out of range) subscript.  Even many C
compilers have optional overflow checking (not perfect, but if turned
on, it will usually catch most buffer overflows within a short time of
them happening).


>> But the point was that the Fortran guys (initially) restricted the
>> forms of the allowed subscript expression in order to help
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>this assisted optimisation ?


See above.


>> >> >In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
>> >> >etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>enough' for my use of FORTRAN.


The point being that the many of the guys who really cared about array
performance, implemented arrays of structures, using subscripts, a
really long time ago.  And that it's not really different if you have
larger or more varied array element sizes (which you had anyway with
multi-dimensional arrays, which, again, were far more commonly used in
Fortran than Cobol).
```

You appear to be confused. Your initial complaint was that COBOL

However, there are other ways. Have you not learnt about GLOBAL and

> >If dynamically CALLed subprograms are used (as I mostly do) then after

…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

Array elements of a single scalar _is_ a different constraint.

later) and allowed any expression to be used as a subscript.

'Quickly' being 25 years for FORTRAN90.

formulated with restrictions to assist the compiler writer while COBOL
was formulated to assist the programmer.

Since then they have moved closer together. FORTRAN changed to improve
programmer flexibility while COBOL changed to improve performance.
Neither are 'faults'.

```
On Nov 23, 4:02 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 09:48:49 -0800 (PST), Richard
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> amount of optimization support for the compiler anyway.

I doubt that indexes were included for "expressiveness"; but rather
to improve performance for both SEARCH and PERFORM VARYING.

I don't understand your reference to "same index value". The content
of an index data item is determined by the implementor. There are
three possiblilities that I see:

A. An occurrence count
B. An occurrence count minus 1
C. An (occurrence count minus 1) times the length of one entry

If either A or B were used, then the same index-name could be used
to access two different arrays; however, if C is used, then the
same index-name could be used only if the length of entries in
both arrays is the same.

[snip]

> Honestly, for a language intended to provide clarity to programmers
> above all else, exposing a trivial micro-optimization like indexes is
> just bizarre.

What may be a "trivial micro-optimization" today is not relevant to
decisions made decades ago. Using occurrence numbers requires
multiplication, relatively inexpensive today, but relatively expensive
then. The COBOL standard, for SEARCH, specifies a "serial operation",
for SEARCH ALL, it specifies a "nonserial operation". Such searchs may
be performed without using multiplication. Not suprisingly, when using
such a method, the index value for a successful search is the same as
C, above.
```

…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>It may well be. That is an implemention issue.


Of course it is.  But the whole notion of indexes exposes what should
be an implementation issue to the programmer.

- **References:** `<o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net> <0kfpc7171lllj4j4k993p2s2kfaleuhphb@4ax.com> <733d570d-92fe-4a5f-a807-47c6eee7afce@d37g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <a6mqc796vj45gau745s2hsi33drer5oir4@4ax.com> <cf1693b4-7e12-49ee-9a7b-f58b89387a36@h3g2000yqa.googlegroups.com> <jnq8d75o55t700v80moqp1k9cik21mje7i@4ax.com>`

```
On Nov 29, 12:27 am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Sat, 26 Nov 2011 14:16:24 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
>
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> just require VARYING unconditionally).

It is not clear what you intend as "Clearly"! <g> In any case,
'SET data-name TO index-name' provides the occurrence number
whenever the progammer wants it.

IBM, as I have heard, was very influential on the CODASYL COBOL
Committee. Indexing was added to COBOL in 1965. The IBM S/360 was
introduced a year or two earlier. Indexing, as implemented by IBM,
fits the S/360 'RX' instructions. Without indexing, an extra
instruction, or more, would be required to get the address, thus
saving, at least, one instruction for every access to a table.
Clearly, improved performance!

> >I don't understand your reference to "same index value". The content
> >of an index data item is determined by the implementor. There are
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>    ADD ABC(INDEX-NAME-ABC) to DEF(INDEX-NAME-DEF).

If one is 'stepping' through tables, one would more likely use:

     SET INDEX-NAME-ABC UP BY 1
     SET INDEX-NAME-DEF UP BY 1

which is much less expensive than SET ... TO ....

From a Programmer's Guide for IBM Full ANS COBOL (Nov 1974):

"Every time a subscripted data-name is referenced in a program, the
compiler generates up to 16 instructions to calculate the correct
displacement. ... Address calculation for a direct index takes a
maximum of four instructions; address calculation for a relative
index takes a few more. Therefore, the use of index-names in
referencing tables is more efficient than the use of
subscripts (sic)". [Index-names are used as subscripts, too!
'Reading IBM manuals cripples the mind ...'. <g>]

> >> Honestly, for a language intended to provide clarity to programmers
> >> above all else, exposing a trivial micro-optimization like indexes is
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> actually had to be stored).

P. J. Plauger, in "The Standard C Library", 1992, provides code
for 'bsearch' that is short and sweet and returns a pointer, but
it still uses multiplication. Again, not as big a deal today as
it was in 1965.
```

```
On Tue, 29 Nov 2011 01:32:26 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Nov 29, 12:27ï¿½am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Sat, 26 Nov 2011 14:16:24 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>whenever the progammer wants it.


I was agreeing - they were clearly added to improve performance, not
the expressiveness of the language.


>IBM, as I have heard, was very influential on the CODASYL COBOL
>Committee. Indexing was added to COBOL in 1965. The IBM S/360 was
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Clearly, improved performance!


IOW, the optimizer was trash.  Maintaining the index "value," can
clearly be done by the compiler, in some fashion approximately
parallel to the value changes of the subscript (obviously not *all*
the time, just at select points where its required to be visible - for
example, looping through subscripts 1 to 10 might compile as a simple
loop incrementing a index value by the appropriate byte offset in each
loop, followed by a final set of the actual subscript value to 10 at
the end of the loop, if it needs to remain visible).  And in fact
that's done often.

Actually that's not quite true - it's almost always done *better* than
that - strength reduction usually reduces to a pointer manipulation in
a loop, and you don't need an index (RX) instruction at all.


>> >I don't understand your reference to "same index value". The content
>> >of an index data item is determined by the implementor. There are
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>which is much less expensive than SET ... TO ....


And again, basic strength reduction will generate that (internally)
from:

    ADD 1 TO SUBSCRIPT
 ï¿½ ï¿½ADD ABC(SUBSCRIPT) to DEF(SUBSCRIPT).


>From a Programmer's Guide for IBM Full ANS COBOL (Nov 1974):
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>'Reading IBM manuals cripples the mind ...'. <g>]


So the optimizer was *documented* to be trash.  Can we go home now?


>> >> Honestly, for a language intended to provide clarity to programmers
>> >> above all else, exposing a trivial micro-optimization like indexes is
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>it was in 1965.


Not exactly true.  Plauger's code, and the C bsearch() is required to
deal with arbitrary length array items - it is, in fact, passed at
runtime, making the multiplication pretty hard to avoid.

This is not the case for the templated C++ binary_search, which can be
passed a constant.  While the template will still generate a
multiplication (possible several, including the ones implied by the
subscripting), the compiler can be trusted to generate a shift/add
sequence (or something) if that's likely to be faster than doing an
actual multiply by a constant.

The compiled SEARCH [ALL] is far closer to the templated C++
binary_search than the bsearch subroutine, as the compiler is
generating the code for it (or should - if the compiler is lame enough
to call a generic subroutine, well...).
```

unnecessary due to advances in technology; but, at best, that
only supports a claim that indexing is archaic - not that it
was a mistake.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-24T02:41:15+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9j4bftFccU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net> <aa21f71f-dacb-40ff-aabd-29a4a39d6f5b@z12g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Nov 23, 1:50 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> What would variables like PH1, PH2.... mean to you?

Easy... Print Heading 1 and Print Heading 2.

(I've seen a LOT of COBOL... :-))


> One of the points
> of Cobol was that it was supposed to be readable by any none
> programmer so PH1, PH2... etc would defeat the object of allowing a
> none programmer to read and understand code.

Not necessarily. Depends how much code the non-programmer has seen and how 
smart he/she is.

Besides, that particular advantage of COBOL was never achieved anyway.

It was only very briefly, and very early on in the development of  COBOL 
that it was touted as being "understandable" by non-programmers. By the 
1970s it was realised that there was much more to it than that.

My favourite quote on this came from an edition of Datamation during the 
70's where they printed an article called "What they really mean" It was 
about the phrases that were starting to appear in Proposals from Vendors...

"Using our new high-level COBOL compilers, computer programs can now be 
written in the English language and your accounting staff can understand 
what programmers are doing."  [What they really mean]  =>  " 02 fltctr 
redefines intzap-field pic s9(7)v9(8) usage is comp-1 value 79.123.

Another of my favourites from the same article...

"The software is in an advanced stage of development and we can deliver 
within the time you require." [What they really mean] => "We'll start 
writing it just as soon as you sign the order."

Unfortunately, I don't have a copy of the article and the above are quoted 
from memory so they may not be exact.



>Also, it needlessly makes
> my job more difficult.

I would argue that unnecessary versbosity makes the job more difficult. The 
more you write, the more chance of error there is. The more complex your 
names are the more chance of mis-remembering them later and getting compiler 
errors.

Certainly, meaningful names in COBOL are a Good Thing, but a name doesn't 
have to be LONG to be meaningful. My single letter "subscripts" are just as 
meaningful to many other people as they are to me. (In all the years I was 
writing COBOL no-one ever complained about having to maintain my code; a 
number of people told me they learned something by maintaining it. Just as I 
learned something from Rick's posts here recently. It isn't just about the 
spelling of names, it is about flair and imagination in implementing 
approaches.

Here's an example...

Which is more meaningful:   ELSE or OTHERWISE?

Both used to be accepted by COBOL but the longer OTHERWISE fell into disuse 
because it takes more writing and adds nothing in terms of meaning; both 
words are meaningful.

Furthermore, when non-programmers who very often can't write coherent 
English anyway, start trying to write English in COBOL... well, it fails at 
a number of levels.

COBOL lulls people into thinking they don't have to be programmers. They 
think in English then write it into COBOL and wonder why their Boolean 
conditions don't work. Impaled on the horns of De Morgan's Laws they just 
make a standard that says NOT cannot be used, or all tests must be positive 
and AND and OR can never be negated. Similarly, they ban nested IFs and 
PERFORM...VARYING rather than understand how these things work from a 
programming point of view and gaining the power of these constructs.

The old joke about "Yesterday I couldn't spell COBOL programmer; today I are 
one..." is not always far of the mark.

Pete.
```

That is invalid COBOL. Thank you for playing.

> errors.

Compiler errors are useful, they tell you that you have done something
wrong.

Lack of compiler errors does not tell you that you have done
everything right.

It is far more useful to be told by the compiler that you have used
the wrong index for a particular table then _NOT_ be told that you
used 'J' in two places where one is performed from another.


> Certainly, meaningful names in COBOL are a Good Thing, but a name doesn't
> have to be LONG to be meaningful. My single letter "subscripts" are just as
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…

They are both identically meaningful. You are confusing this with
familiarity.

You are familiar with 'J', 'K', etc because you have used them for
years and you assume that familiarity and meaning are special
attributes of those letters.

What is easy to understand is entirely dependent on what you are used
to. It is wrong of you to extrapolate your 40 years of usage to some
special magic insight.

Many people, especially those who have been programming for decades,
may well understand, or determine quickly, that I thru N (from
FORTRAN) are fixed point integers. But you have extended that
(according to you messages) to allow them to be reused wherever a
local variable should be used. Apart from this being poor practice for
global variables, there is nothing inherent in the name (as you assume
there is), that indicates how these should be restricted, or at least
systematically checked to ensure that reuse is possible.

For example if I have:

      PERFORM VARYING J FROM 1 BY 1 ....
           blah blah
           PERFORM Check-Characters
           blah blah
      END-PERFORM

Then it is necessary, under your stated practices, to check that Check-
Characters does not use J.

You, of course, being familiar with your own code, will 'know' whether
it does or not. Other programmers do not.


> Both used to be accepted by COBOL but the longer OTHERWISE fell into disuse
> because it takes more writing and adds nothing in terms of meaning; both
> words are meaningful.

Poor example. Whether people write OTHERWISE or not is irrelevant to
the fact that reading it is just as easy because it is a common word
in english usage.

> English anyway, start trying to write English in COBOL... well, it fails at
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

This is different from other languages in what way ?

BASIC, especially the way that Visual-BASIC and Office BASIC was
promoted, is just the same.

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>


They were variables where PH were the initials of the programmer.



> (I've seen a LOT of COBOL... :-))

> one..." is not always far of the mark.

That may all be due to people paying too much attention to dumbing
down the skill levels of programmers; probably as a result of not
being able to retain skilled staff (usually because the environment is

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

_(thread depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-24T15:07:19+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9j5n6pFflrU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net> <aa21f71f-dacb-40ff-aabd-29a4a39d6f5b@z12g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <9j4bftFccU1@mid.individual.net> <964eb99e-d93c-4a8d-bd48-fd54b9b7ef6c@h42g2000yqd.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Nov 23, 1:41 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> They were variables where PH were the initials of the programmer.

My response was tongue-in-cheek... :-)

Sure... and they could have been ANYTHING when you take them out of context. 
I think looking at the definition and the context where they are used would 
give you a fair idea of what they are for. It doesn't actually MATTER what 
they are called; it is how and where they are used that matters.

>
>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> shite or the wages suitable only for slaves).

If you consider "dumbing down" (or, more kindly, "simplifying") to be bad, 
are you in favour of the alternative? :-)

Restrictive standards don't dumb down the skills of programmers. (Having 
worked in such places I can attest that my level of skill was unaffected; 
however my level of annoyance and frustration increased dramatically...:-)) 
Good programmers are always seeking to improve their skills, learn new 
stuff, experiment, and become more proficient. They do this whether their 
workplace encourages it or not, because the first and highest priority must 
be to your personal and professional development, (you will never get out of 
a rotten job unless you rise above it...), and is not necessarily to the 
company where you work. (Although, you owe them your loyalty as long as they 
are paying you...)

 If the environment is shite and slave wages are being paid, then people 
won't work there. (If they do, then they are being suckered by their 
employers. Don't blame the employers, they're just short sighted idiots who 
don't deserve your time; put the responsibility where it belongs... on the 
suckers. If no-one came to work, the company would have to review its 
policy...) There is a choice. If someone allows themselves to be persuaded 
that they have no choice then that is not really a problem with the 
workplace.

How you perceive things has a huge effect on whether you enjoy them or not.

During my own career there have been several watersheds where everything I 
had learned was made obsolete and I might as well have not learned it. At 
least, that's how it seemed to me at the time. With the benefit of hindsight 
and sitting in the comfort of my armchair now, I realise that that 
perception was neither true nor fair. The action of learning ANYTHING 
requires a discipline and application which are very valuable attributes to 
embrace. You learn not to quit. That is a much more important lesson than 
memorizing VSAM return codes, or knowing all the parameters for a SQL 
COALESCE... :-)

I had someone here yesterday and we had a problem with a scratched DVD.  No 
amount of cleaning (the player or the disk) seemed to fix it. My guest was 
ready to quit after a few minutes but I told her I couldn't. She suggested 
we watch something else. I declined. (However, I did agree that if it wasn't 
fixed in 15 minutes, I'd go with that.) Eight minutes later it was working 
perfectly. She commented on my tenacity. (One man's tenacity is another's 
obstinacy :-)) and we enjoyed the movie. The point is that she has grown up 
in a culture where if you don't succeed at first, it goes in the "too hard" 
basket. I don't have a "too hard" basket; I have  "possible" or "impossible" 
baskets and nothing goes in the "impossible" basket unless it can be clearly 
shown to be impossible. (Not knowing how to do it does not qualify it for 
the impossible basket; knowledge can be acquired...)

If you are a COBOL programmer (or indeed, ANY language programmer) and you 
work on a site where they restrict the way you can use it or "dumb down" the 
standard approaches, would you let that stop your professional development? 
While you have to comply with what they want, you don't have to let it 
stifle you. Although your job is important, it doesn't define you as a 
person and if you let a suppressive company do that then you are just 
selling yourself short. Improve your skills, change your attitude, get a 
better job, or, at the very least, ensure the job you are in is not stunting 
your growth...

Pete.
```

In article <abb5f526-88ac-4f2d-b823-ad2efb0b7f61@c18g2000yqj.googlegroups.com>,
	Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com> writes:
> On Nov 23, 1:32ï¿½am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> > > I don't know where you got "J thru P", nor which other languages
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> where integer variables were I thru N.

I hate to disagree with you, but here is the relevant section from the
Microsoft GW-Basic Manual:

========================================================

6.2.2 Type Declaration Characters

Type declaration characters indicate what a variable represents. The
following type declaration characters are recognized:
Character	Type of Variable
$	String variable
%	Integer variable
!	Single-precision variable
#	Double-precision variable

The following are sample variable names for each type:
Variable Type	Sample Name
String variable	N$
Integer variable	LIMIT%
Single-precision variable	MINIMUM!
Double-precision variable	Pl#

The default type for a numeric variable name is single-precision.
Double-precision, while very accurate, uses more memory space and
more calculation time. Single-precision is sufficiently accurate
for most applications. However, the seventh significant digit (if
printed) will not always be accurate. You should be very careful
when making conversions between integer, single-precision, and
double-precision variables.

The following variable is a single-precision value by default:

ABC

Variables beginning with FN are assumed to be calls to a user-defined
function.

The GW-BASIC statements DEFINT, DEFSTR, DEFSNG, and DEFDBL may be
included in a program to declare the types of values for certain
variable names.

========================================================


And that pretty much mirrors the methods used by all but a few toy BASICs
I have used in my long career. (Some, like NorthStar, used different "Type
declaration characters" but the concept remained the same.

bill
```

```
On Nov 23, 11:44 pm, Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.macl...@gmail.com>
wrote:
> On Nov 23, 1:32 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> long."

Then all numeric variables were of the same type (integer) and no
variable names were special to give a different type. This fails to be
an example of anything, let alone the claim.

> I used to use a version of BASIC (TELCOMP?) and subsequently GW-BASIC
> where integer variables were I thru N.

TELCOMP is not BASIC, it derives from JOSS. There is no indication
that it uses special characters to determine variable type. JEAN,
which is another derivative of JOSS certainly did not (I just checked
my manual).

You are wrong. GW-BASIC does not use letters in the variable name to
make that variable a particular type. You can coerce any variable to
be integer by using a "%" at the end, or to string using "$". There is
no special meaning to names I to N.

If you wrote programs using variables I to N as integer then it was
purely by convention after learning to do so in FORTRAN. There is no
language support for this in BASIC.
```

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 10:06:15 -0800 (PST), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>You are wrong. GW-BASIC does not use letters in the variable name to
>make that variable a particular type. You can coerce any variable to
>be integer by using a "%" at the end, or to string using "$". There is
>no special meaning to names I to N.


I'd quibble with you use of the word coerce.  A, A% and A$ were
different variables in most implementations of BASIC, so the suffix
was both the type and part of the name, and did not provide and sort
of coercion (IOW "A = B + C$" was not a valid expression in most
cases).  For numeric types, appropriate conversions in expressions did
occur.


>If you wrote programs using variables I to N as integer then it was
>purely by convention after learning to do so in FORTRAN. There is no
>language support for this in BASIC.


I agree, I've never seen a BASIC that did typing that way by default,
although some had a mechanism for doing something like that.  GW-BASIC
(at least later versions), for example, allowed you to add a "DEFINT
I-N" statement at the top of your program, in case you were an escaped
Fortran programmer.  There were also DEFDBL, DEFSTR and the like.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-23T11:52:00-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3bc440eb-d629-44bf-bcb8-2c8dced6dc37@r9g2000vbw.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <a35c4935-d3b0-4730-9c2c-cbc823e9774d@h30g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <x7KdnehXMqqpXzXTnZ2dnUVZ_qCdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7e8baa4c-b383-4b0b-9ea7-9cfe7d513c5d@q39g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <9ZidnXpLONzbRDTTnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <oe6mc7pappl2bggr3cjpb8jbd46nu93qvt@4ax.com> <9j1b0qFcqmU1@mid.individual.net> <ab4750c4-a540-4b6b-aabe-8e784bec03c3@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <9j30haFf5mU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Nov 22, 8:28 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > On Nov 22, 5:14 am, "Pete Dashwood"
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> > (with or without offset).


> >>>> Yes, I do that too, inasmuch as I always use INDEXED BY and provide
> >>>> the required index as a unique index used ONLY by THAT table.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> context under which I use the terms and it is enough for me.

What? No "fierce debate"? Perhaps then, some levity!

I am tempted to quote Lewis Carroll, but I will resist. <g>

The "meaning" of indexing and subscripting can be reversed.

=====
      $set remove "index"
       identification division.
       program-id. idx-v-ss.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       1 a-table value "13579".
        2 some-entries occurs 5
           ascending key is integer
           indexed by subscript.
         3 integer pic 9.
       77 index comp pic 9(4).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           set subscript to 1
           search some-entries
               varying subscript
           at end
               continue
           when integer (subscript) > 4
               continue
           end-search
           set index to subscript
           display integer (index)
           stop run
           .
=====

In the above program, I use "subscript" as an index
and use "index" as a subscript, informally. However, in
terms of the COBOL language, "subscript" is an index-name
used as a subscript and "index" is a data-name used as a
subscript.

"For a large class of cases - though not for all - in which
we employ the word "meaning" it can be defined thus: the
meaning of a word is its use in the language."
--Ludwig Wittgenstein, Philosophical Investigations, pt. 1,
  sct. 43 (1953). (Copied from a secondary source.)

"Subscripting" is discussed in the 2002 standard in
"E.2.4 Subscripting". "Indexing" is discussed in
"E.2.4.1 Subscripting using index-names".

Enough said!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels (and importance of source code)

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-22T14:23:21+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9j0bs9FpprU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <a35c4935-d3b0-4730-9c2c-cbc823e9774d@h30g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <x7KdnehXMqqpXzXTnZ2dnUVZ_qCdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7e8baa4c-b383-4b0b-9ea7-9cfe7d513c5d@q39g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <9ZidnXpLONzbRDTTnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com>`

```
john@wexfordpress.com wrote:
<snip>>
> First and last COBOL should be readable.

No John, first and last COBOL (like all programming languages) should 
produce correct results.

Readability is something that mattered when source code maintenance 
mattered. In the world of objects, source code is not particularly important 
and the language that a given Class is written in is irrelevant (as long as 
the functionality provided is what is wanted). It is VERY hard for COBOL 
people to get their heads around this.

I guess if you think about all the functionality you use regularly and DON'T 
have the soure code for, it might help. (OS, compiler, sorts, third party 
software, etc.) Windows OS has around 800 components you can use and the 
.Net Framework has around 100,000. You don't have source code for any of 
them... It doesn't make them any less useful.

When developing Classes (instances of which are objects) it is wise to keep 
them small. They are automatically encapsulated, so the problem of 
maintenance is minimised before you start. It is not hard to replace a Class 
if you don't like what it does (or you can override it or extend the methods 
in it if you wrote it yourself and have the source), but it is actually 
possible to extend classes for which you don't have the source. (You simply 
"wrap" them and add your own methods.)

The world of objects and layers is quite different from the world of 
procedural COBOL. I have products I offer for sale which are written in 
three different  languages (C#, COBOL, and WSH) and it is isn't a problem 
for the clients or for me. Classes can be written in the language that best 
suits that Class and it is all equal at run time on the level playing field 
provided by .Net.

I buy and use components all the time for which I don't have source code and 
I have supplied components to other people without giving them source code.

Even legacy COBOL components (for which I DO have the source code) can be 
replaced (rather than "maintained") and I am gradually phasing some of these 
out and replacing them. (The trouble is that they are so robust and reliable 
I don't like throwing away things that are not broken... :-))


> If independent data items are
> given 77 levels and grouped at the beginning of WORKING STORAGE
> SECTION  then finding them is a bit easier.
> But it is not a biggie either way.
>
Sure, I agree.

I think I moved to preferring 01 levels when I got into SQL. SQL  data 
declarations ALWAYS work of they are 01 levels; they SOMETIMES work if they 
are not. (Depends on the RDBMS). When it comes to generating SQL code, I use 
01 levels because I need all the help I can get...:-) Making host variables 
01 means I have one thing less to worry about.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-af3b3c03df"></a>
## Thread: ODO Demo — 14 msgs

### ODO Demo

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-02T22:12:48-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<486e37ce-ba54-49c3-86a5-860968934671@eh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```

This is a program to demonstrate the use of ODO with sort
and intrinsic functions. The Micro Focus compiler used dates
from 1994, but did receive some updates in 1995.

For this simple sort, the SORT statement matches the 2002
standard.

Note that loops and intemediate data items would be required
to obtain the same results as the sort and intrinsic functions.
This would turn 54 lines into several hundred.

=====
       identification division.
       program-id. odo-demo.
      *demonstrates ODO with sort and intrinsic functions
       data division.
       77 odo-count comp pic 9(4) value 10.
       1 odo-group.
        2 odo-table
           occurs 1 to 10 times
           depending on odo-count.
         3 odo-entry pic 9.
       77 result pic z9.
       procedure division.
       begin.
           initialize odo-group
           move 5 to odo-count
           move 9 to odo-entry (1)
           move 7 to odo-entry (2)
           move 5 to odo-entry (3)
           move 3 to odo-entry (4)
           move 1 to odo-entry (5)
           perform display-entries
           sort odo-table
               ascending odo-entry
           perform display-entries
           perform intrinsic-functions
           stop run
           .

       display-entries.
           display odo-entry (1) space
                   odo-entry (2) space
                   odo-entry (3) space
                   odo-entry (4) space
                   odo-entry (5)
           .

       intrinsic-functions.
           compute result = function max (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "max"
           compute result = function mean (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "mean"
           compute result = function median (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "median"
           compute result = function midrange (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "midrange"
           compute result = function ord-max (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "ord-max"
           compute result = function ord-min (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "ord-min"
           compute result = function range (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "range"
           compute result = function sum (odo-entry (all))
           display result space "sum"
           .
=====
9 7 5 3 1
1 3 5 7 9
 9 max
 5 mean
 5 median
 5 midrange
 5 ord-max
 1 ord-min
 8 range
25 sum
=====
```

```
On Nov 3, 8:08 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > This is a program to demonstrate the use of ODO with sort
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
[Program snipped]
>
> Good job, Rick!
>
> It is impressive.

Perhaps you might clarify what you mean by impressive,
or not. <g>

There may be some who may think "I forgot about that"
or "I didn't know that" or "I could have used ...",
but I can't think of anything that would rise to the
level of "impressive".

Anyway, it seems this a continuation of comments made
in March 2008 while discussing ODO. The lead-in was
the value of ODO when using SEARCH and SEARCH ALL.

< http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/484596fb50366bc8?hl=en
>
=====

"Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:13teonpobo7kve4@corp.supernews.com...
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net...

>> Next point?

> Intrinsic functions using the ALL subscript will work with
> ODO tables, but likely will not give the correct result when
> using fixed length tables with a variable number of items.

> MAX, MEAN, MEDIAN, MIDRANGE, MIN,
> ORD-MAX, ORD-MIN, PRESENT-VALUE,
> RANGE, STANDARD-DEVIATION, SUM, and
> VARIANCE are those affected.

Fair enough.

Again, facilities I wouldn't use often enough to lose sleep
over... :-)

I don't think I've ever used ALL as a subscript, and MAX, MIN, and SUM
seem
more like SQL to me than COBOL... (I have used those in SQL...). So it
looks
to me as if my COBOL use may be "limited", notwithstanding the fact I
used
it successfully for decades. :-)

=====

For my part, assessing the value ODO for SEARCH and
SEARCH ALL is old and trivial, in comparison with its
value for intrinsic functions.

The bottom-line, simply, is that wholesale rejection
of ODO makes parts of COBOL unavailable, while general
acceptance of ODO gives access to all parts of the
language. Therefore, use of ODO should be encouraged;
not discouraged. <g>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ODO Demo

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-05T12:13:40+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9hj9t5Fu1U1@mid.individual.net>`

…[17 more quoted lines elided]…

resisted the removal of ALTER from the language, even though by then I had 
no use for it myself. I don't think ANYTHING should be 'unavailable' but I 
am allowed to have an opinion about what I think is useful or not. (And 
others are perfectly free to disagree; it is a discussion forum, not Holy 
Writ or dogma)

Rejecting (or embracing) ODO use is a matter for individuals, just like 
everything else. I have never advocated removing it from the language.

You have shown (very persuasively) that there are some occasions where ODO 
can be useful.

My position has been shifted to the point where I must grant that. :-)

However, I still wouldn't use it. The occasions you have presented are cases 
which I would rarely use and I already have adequate means of dealing with 
them in COBOL, which do not require use of ODO.

In general, I consider it a useless construct that simply causes more 
trouble than it is worth.

But that, (as was recently pointed out) is merely opinion.:-), and, having 
seen your code, I am not as strongly persuaded against it as I was.

Pete.

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> OK. When I looked through the code you posted I was impressed. It is
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

I can't see much use for ODO inasmuch as the compiler reserves as much room 
as the largest entry; with one exception.

If you desire to write a variable length record (at least in Fujitsu) it 
comes in handy. Without it, the FJ compiler writes the full record, padded 
with spaces. For example:

01  MYREC.
  02  MYDATA            PIC X(10000).

MOVE 'A' TO MYDATA.
WRITE MYREC.

Will write a 10,000 byte record. Whereas:

01  MYREC.
  02  MYDATA.
    05  FILLER OCCURS 1 TO 10000 DEPENDING ON RECORD-LEN PIC X.

MOVE 1 TO RECORD-LEN.
MOVE 'A' TO MYDATA
WRITE MYREC

writes a 1-byte record.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ODO Demo

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-08T09:10:54-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8d404ac2-e943-4ce3-b609-57d227edce72@u5g2000vbd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<486e37ce-ba54-49c3-86a5-860968934671@eh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <9hfi1sFtqoU1@mid.individual.net> <130409a5-15e7-4c3c-a43a-3ab2983575bf@x1g2000yqg.googlegroups.com> <9hj9t5Fu1U1@mid.individual.net> <E_2dncwJd8REOCnTnZ2dnUVZ_h2dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On Nov 4, 9:56 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:

> I can't see much use for ODO inasmuch as the compiler reserves as much room
> as the largest entry; with one exception.

"compiler reserves" is a misnomer when it is, in fact,
"programmer reserves" and without exception.

There are two uses of ODO to consider: table of records
and table of characters.

For table of records, ODO will limit the number of elements
examined for SEARCH, SEARCH ALL, SORT (2002), and intrinsic
functions.

For table of characters (or character array), ODO will limit
the size of the data item when used as a sending or receiving
field for MOVE, STRING, UNSTRING, DISPLAY, etc.

> If you desire to write a variable length record (at least in Fujitsu) it
> comes in handy. Without it, the FJ compiler writes the full record, padded
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> writes a 1-byte record.

The COBOL standard (1985) has the RECORD VARYING clause
to address this issue, but despite the similarities there
is no connection to ODO.

=====
       identification division.
       program-id. rec-var.
       environment division.
       input-output section.
       file-control.
           select var-file
               assign to "var-file.dat".
       data division.
       file section.
       fd var-file
           record varying
               from 1 to 10000
               depending on rec-len.
       1 myrec.
        2 mydata pic x(10000).
       working-storage section.
       77 rec-len comp pic 9(8) value 10000.
       procedure division.
       begin.
           open output var-file
           move "A" to mydata
           move 1 to rec-len
           write myrec
           close var-file
           stop run
           .
=====

This program writes a one character record without using ODO.

So, I guess from your initial statement you "can't
see much use for ODO" at all. <g>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ODO Demo

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-08T10:30:55-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<228b26d7-a183-4126-a579-452e8dda3da0@t38g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<486e37ce-ba54-49c3-86a5-860968934671@eh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <9hfi1sFtqoU1@mid.individual.net> <130409a5-15e7-4c3c-a43a-3ab2983575bf@x1g2000yqg.googlegroups.com> <9hj9t5Fu1U1@mid.individual.net> <E_2dncwJd8REOCnTnZ2dnUVZ_h2dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On Nov 5, 3:56 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> as the largest entry; with one exception.

Different people may see different things.

In other languages where there is dynamic memory the compiler usually
reserves an area called the heap which is used for data items as they
are created. This can be added to from system or virtual memory if
required. There is a cost in allocating this memory (alloc()) to
objects and a large cost in making objects larger (realloc()). This
heap can also become fragmented after many alloc()s and free()s
leading to wasted memory.

In some language systems there is a need for garbage collection which,
again, has a processing overhead, but without which the memory leakage
would become excessive.

OTOH ODO has almost zero processing overhead.


> If you desire to write a variable length record (at least in Fujitsu) it
> comes in handy. Without it, the FJ compiler writes the full record, padded
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> WRITE MYREC.

If you are talking about LINE SEQUENTIAL files then perhaps you
haven't discovered the environment variable:

    CBR_TRAILING_BLANK_RECORD=REMOVE

which, for Fujitsu COBOL since version 3, causes the program to only
write the length of record up to the last non-blank (plus line
terminator).

> Will write a 10,000 byte record. Whereas:
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> writes a 1-byte record.
```
