<a id="t-a03a125977"></a>
## Thread: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol — 4 this month / 26 total

### INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

- **From:** rtwolfe78@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2011-11-28T10:19:47-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10>`

```
COBOL Fans....Check out the Infoworld article which states that COBOL is one of the 7 programming languages on the rise.

To paraphrase Mr. Samuel Clemons, a.k.a.Mark Twain:  "...the reports of COBOL's death are greatly exaggerated."

URL:
http://www.infoworld.com/d/developer-world/7-programming-languages-the-rise-620?page=0,4&source=fssr

.......here is the excerpt from the article regarding COBOL:

"Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

It may not be fair to call Cobol a niche language as it was once the dominant language in the enterprise. Grace Murray Hopper, famous for finding the first bug in the early mainframes, helped create the language in 1959 and it's been enhanced hundreds of times since. Cobol jockeys today get to play with object-oriented extensions, self-modifying code, and practically every other gimmick.

That never earned it much respect in some circles. Or as famous academic Edsger Dijkstra put it: "The use of Cobol cripples the mind; its teaching should, therefore, be regarded as a criminal offense." The folks in mainframe shops everywhere ignored this note and soldiered on. IBM calls one of the latest releases "Enterprise Cobol 4.2," but it could as easily be numbered 147.2 or maybe even 588.3. Cobol programmers like the syntax that's more like a natural language with actual nouns and verbs that form clauses and sentences -- a technique that might call Ruby to mind.

While fewer schools are teaching new programmers Cobol, the language is far from dying, with many corporations continuing to invest in their Cobol stacks. A recent search of Dice.com showed 580 jobs mentioning Cobol and 1,070 mentioning Ruby. The bulk of the jobs seemed to involve counting money ("asset management") and counting doctor's visits ("Health IT"). While these are some of the same areas that first adopted computers for back-office processing, the work still needs to be done.

Versions of the languages run on JVMs and .Net virtual machines making it possible to migrate code stacks away from mainframes to Linux boxes. Programmers who want to use a more modern IDE can search for plug-ins to Eclipse, a project that is gaining new support."
```

#### ↳ Re: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-29T14:27:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9jiqosFaqpU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10>`

```
rtwolfe78@gmail.com wrote:
> COBOL Fans....Check out the Infoworld article which states that COBOL
> is one of the 7 programming languages on the rise.
>
> To paraphrase Mr. Samuel Clemons, a.k.a.Mark Twain:  "...the reports
> of COBOL's death are greatly exaggerated."

Mark Twain IS dead and reports of his death are NOT exaggerated.
>
> URL:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> self-modifying code, and practically every other gimmick.

About 1% of them do. Most of them see Object Orientation as an unnecessary 
re-invention of Modular Programming. I have never seen a COBOL shop (and I 
have worked in many) where "self-modifiying code" was permitted by site 
standards, and the obvious self-modifying construct (ALTER) was deleted from 
the language due to a consensus of lobbying by people who had no idea how to 
use it properly or effectively.
 As for "practically every other gimmick..."... this is a meaningless 
statement that is predicated on the use of Object COBOL, which most COBOL 
programmers avoid like the plague. Please cite ONE single "gimmick" that has 
been added to COBOL in the last 20 years (and actually implemented by a 
COBOL vendor) and is NOT Object based. The sentence above is written by a 
journalist who has no idea what COBOL programming is about.


 >
> That never earned it much respect in some circles. Or as famous
> academic Edsger Dijkstra put it: "The use of Cobol cripples the mind;
> its teaching should, therefore, be regarded as a criminal offense."

Dijkstra has been oft-quoted on something he actually said with 
tongue-in-cheek. Learning COBOL does not cripple the mind, but it may 
over-simplify what is possible with programming and discourage people from 
finding out more. (the famous: "Everything I want to do I can do in COBOL" 
It indicates a limited vision rather than a crippled mind.)

> The folks in mainframe shops everywhere ignored this note and
> soldiered on. IBM calls one of the latest releases "Enterprise Cobol
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> technique that might call Ruby to mind.

As most of the people writing it don't speak English as a first language, 
similarity to THEIR natural language may not be important.

It does encourage over-importance of source code (which IS important for 
COBOL shops, but is not so important for the rest of the world.)
>
> While fewer schools are teaching new programmers Cobol, the language
> is far from dying, with many corporations continuing to invest in
> their Cobol stacks. A recent search of Dice.com showed 580 jobs
> mentioning Cobol and 1,070 mentioning Ruby.

Would you consider Ruby and COBOL to be similar?

I wonder how recent that was? And "mentioning COBOL" doesn't mean they are 
offering a COBOL job. I just checked Jobserve and it returned 36 COBOL jobs 
for UK and Europe, and 45 for the USA. Some of these were thngs like "C++ 
programmer with COBOL experience"... (the optimism of some of these agencies 
never ceases to amaze me :-)) and many of them were primarily for Java but 
they would like it if you knew some COBOL. Having said that, there were some 
jobs that are definitely for COBOL and they are mainly mainframe. Columbus 
Ohio seems to have a number of COBOL requirements but the rates are not 
good.

Nevertheless, I would agree there has been a slight increase in COBOL jobs 
over the last year.

In the last two years COBOL has moved up from around 30th most used 
programming language to 24th so there is definitely an upswing.

I have a theory about this but I'll leave it for now.

> The bulk of the jobs
> seemed to involve counting money ("asset management") and counting
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> for plug-ins to Eclipse, a project that is gaining new support."

Eclipse? New? COBOL has been running on it for at least 7 years.

The fairest conclusion that can be drawn is that COBOL is rising in 
interest, but it still has a long way to go before it is worth investing in.

The "good stuff" in COBOL requires Object support, just as it does for any 
other modern programming language.

That isn't a problem and IBM (mid-range; I don't know about their current 
mainframe compiler...), Micro Focus, and Fujitsu all offer Object support in 
their COBOL compilers. Both Fujitsu and Micro Focus made a lot of noise 
about producing an IL generating compiler for .Net which is fully Object 
Oriented. (And these compilers are very good, largely because they 
effectively harness the power of the .Net framework through their respective 
IDEs (Eclipse and Visual Studio).  Unfortunately, they are also expensive 
and the Micro Focus one requires run time fees...)

There are two problems with this solution, though.

1. You don't need to generate IL code to run OO COBOL on .Net. (If you have 
an existing Fujitsu NetCOBOL or MicroFocus Net Express compiler, that's all 
you need. The .Net Framework provides a special service to run this code 
alongside .Net code. It actually runs faster than .Net IL/CLR code. 
Interaction between .Net objects and Native objects is seamless and works 
very well. I've been doing it for a number of years now and find no trouble 
at all with it. C# Classes talk to COBOL and COBOL talks to C#; neither of 
them cares what language the object they are talking to is written in. This 
is what I mean when I say that source code is much less relevant in 
non-COBOL environments.)

2. If you REALLY want IL code there are FREE compilers for .Net languages 
that can do that and they come with a free copy of Eclipse or Visual Studio.

I see the following adoption pattern (this is just one of many possible 
scenarios... I'm not saying it is definitive, but most end-points do not 
include COBOL):

1. Existing COBOL shop decides .Net is the place to be. Contacts their 
current vendor who sells them an IL generating compiler and a few ancilliary 
tools. Kerch-ching!

2. COBOL guys haven't had a bone for a very long time so they are excited to 
be able to use .Net objects and Classes. The new COBOL is pretty sexy and 
works very well.

3. A year or so later the programmers have got a pretty good idea of how 
objects work and they are starting to realise that thousands of lines of 
code that need maintenance is not a good thing for the company or for them. 
Some of them start looking at C# or VB.Net...(And COBOL still does not look 
good on a CV...)

4. Five years on the COBOL has fallen by the wayside and been largely 
replaced with the free compilers and IDEs.

The point is that COBOL has no long term future and it isn't just because of 
fashion. (However, you can continue using it just as you could keep digging 
with a stick after shovels were invented.)

It is interesting to me that the recent enquiries to PRIMA are NOT coming 
from COBOL people. Certainly last year it was mainly COBOL people who knew 
COBOL, who were talking to us. That seems to have changed. Now it is people 
who are either advising companies how to reduce dependence on COBOL or it is 
IT guys who know all about objects and layers (but not about COBOL) and 
heard we have a solution for COBOL legacy that implements that and allows 
Legacy AND New technology to co-exist on the same platform, accessing the 
same database, without needing middleware solutions that lock you in.

Pete.
```

## Thread: Direct client req : Sr. Business Analyst at Baltimore, MD a Multiple Years Position. — 3 this month / 12 total

_Topics: [`Jobs, careers, recruiting, salary`](topics.md#jobs)_

### Direct client req : Sr. Business Analyst at Baltimore, MD a Multiple Years Position.

- **From:** Vishnu Vardhan <vvardhan@netvisionresources.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-29T12:28:30-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0e52c3a2-fe47-47f7-ba14-1eb170a8cbad@o9g2000vbc.googlegroups.com>`

```
Sr. Business Analyst (Subject Matter Expert)
This position is a Multiple Year Contract position and is located at
Baltimore, MD

QUALIFICATIONS
Our client is in a need of a Senior Level Business Analyst with Cobol
Atleast 8+ years of  technical experience in the field directly
related to the required area of expertise.
The candidate must have hands on experience with:
Knowledge of COBOL, CICS, JCL, VSAM, DB2 environment
Job Description:
Client is looking for a Business Analyst to assist the client with
ICD-10 remediation project. Candidate will assist team of 15 technical
analysts, programmers, and testers in developing and implementing a
solution for the legacy mainframe application.
• Knowledge of COBOL, CICS, JCL, VSAM, DB2 environment.
• Elicit requirements using interviews, document analysis,
requirements workshops, surveys, site visits, business process
descriptions, use cases, scenarios, business analysis, task and
workflow analysis.
• Critically evaluate information gathered from multiple sources,
reconcile conflicts, decompose high-level information into details,
abstract up from low-level information to a general understanding, and
distinguish user requests from the underlying true needs.
• Proactively communicate and collaborate with external and internal
customers to analyze information needs and functional requirements and
deliver the following artifacts as needed.
• Utilize your experience in using enterprise-wide requirements
definition and management systems and methodologies required.
• Strong analytical and product management skills required, including
a thorough understanding of how to interpret customer business needs
and translate them into application and operational requirements.
• Excellent verbal and written communication skills and the ability to
interact professionally with a diverse group, government client,
managers, programmers and testers.
• Develop requirements specifications according to standard templates,
using natural language.
• Collaborate with developers and subject matter experts to establish
the technical vision and analyze tradeoffs between usability and
performance needs.

Preferred skills: Previous health care environment experience.


Interested contact
```

```
On Nov 29, 3:28 pm, Vishnu Vardhan <vvard...@netvisionresources.com>
wrote:
> Sr. Business Analyst (Subject Matter Expert)
> QUALIFICATIONS
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Knowledge of COBOL, CICS, JCL, VSAM, DB2 environment

Hmmmmm... this deserves a close reading from which we can cull out
some industry trends.

Note how the Position Title is _Sr. Business Analyst (Subject Matter
Expert)_, or _Senior Level Business Analyst with Cobol_.
And yet, they are seeking "technical experience... hands on experience
with: Knowledge of COBOL, CICS, JCL, VSAM, DB2...".

What I am noticing more and more is that COI / Top Management's
refusal to admit, or take any responsibility for, many bad decisions
in the past involving outsourcing, offshoring, turning "personnel
departments" into "human resource departments", gutting training
programs, basing decisions on lowest-cost hourly rates and not on true-
cost-of-development-and-ownership, etc. etc., all gets manifested in
*gosh*, Let's Just Redefine the Roles!!

This post shows the ultimate absurdity, and the near-impossibility of
educating  managers... Why, if the projects of the past have failed it
must be because the Business Subject Matter Expert didn't know CICS
Internals!

Another probable "justification" for this type of post is that the
client, like most IT clients nowadays, in fact denigrates "technical
types", and after all, programmers/coders are a dime a dozen, and
their rates are set according to this derisive attitude.

Ahhh, what they really need is a programmer/developer who knows his
stuff! But hey, can't find anybody at the proverbial insulting $35-$40/
hour rate that the CIO allows, so we'll just up-scale him to "Business
Analyst." The CIO says we can charge more for them!

Such madness truly knows no bounds... the horror... the horror of it
all...

Note to self: Modify resume accordingly. I am now a Business Subject
Matter Expert with CoBOL experience, rather than a CoBOL developer
with application subject matter expertise.

Ken
```
